## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |

| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# big-code-pg-arch-001: PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline

## Task

Trace the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline from parse to execute. Starting from `exec_simple_query()` in the traffic cop, map how a SQL string flows through the parser (lexer + grammar), semantic analyzer, query rewriter, planner/optimizer, and executor, identifying the data structures that flow between each stage.

## Context

- **Repository**: github.com/sg-evals/postgres--5a461dc4 (mirror of postgres/postgres) (C, ~1.5M LOC)
- **Category**: Architectural Understanding
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: src/backend/ — parser, rewrite, optimizer, executor subsystems

## Requirements

1. Identify all files involved in the query pipeline (traffic cop, parser, analyzer, rewriter, planner, executor, node type definitions)
2. Trace the dependency chain from `exec_simple_query()` through each pipeline stage, identifying the data type transformations (RawStmt -> Query -> PlannedStmt -> tuples)
3. Document the two-phase optimization (Path generation via allpaths.c, then Plan creation via createplan.c)
4. Explain the Volcano-style executor dispatch in execProcnode.c

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — role in architecture
- path/to/file2.ext — role in architecture
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Entry point: path/to/entry.ext
2. Calls: path/to/next.ext (via function/method name)
3. Delegates to: path/to/impl.ext
...

## Analysis
[Detailed architectural analysis including:
- Design patterns identified
- Component responsibilities
- Data flow description
- Interface contracts between components]

## Summary
[Concise 2-3 sentence summary answering the task question]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- File recall: Did you find the correct set of architecturally relevant files?
- Dependency accuracy: Did you trace the correct dependency/call chain?
- Architectural coherence: Did you correctly identify the design patterns and component relationships?

412026-03-11T18:07:55.080104agentmessage-
I am thinking...: Now I have a comprehensive picture of the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline. Let me synthesize all the information I've gathered:

1. **Traffic cop** (src/backend/tcop/postgres.c): `exec_simple_query()` is the entry point. It:
   - Calls `pg_parse_query()` to get a List of RawStmt*
   - Calls `pg_analyze_and_rewrite_fixedparams()` to get a List of Query*
   - Calls `pg_plan_queries()` to get a List of PlannedStmt*
   - Creates a portal and calls `PortalRun()` to execute

2. **Parser** (src/backend/parser/):
   - `pg_parse_query()` → `raw_parser()` (parser.c)
   - `raw_parser()` initializes the flex scanner (`scanner_init` → scan.l) and bison parser (`base_yyparse` → gram.y)
   - Returns List<RawStmt*>

   - Returns Query*

   - `pg_rewrite_query()` → `QueryRewrite()` 
   - Expands rules, views, row security policies
   - Returns List<Query*>

- `pg_analyze_and_rewrite_fixedparams()` → `parse_analyze_fixedparams()` → `transformTopLevelStmt()`

**Stage 3 — Query Rewriting** (`rewrite/rewriteHandler.c`):
- `pg_rewrite_query()` → `QueryRewrite()` — expands views, fires rules, injects row security
- Produces **`List<Query*>`** (one input query may expand to multiple)

**Stage 4 — Two-Phase Optimization** (`optimizer/plan/`):
- **Phase 1 (allpaths.c)**: `make_one_rel()` generates all cost-annotated `Path` alternatives for base rels and joins via dynamic programming (DP in `joinrels.c`, or GEQO for large join sets)
- **Phase 2 (createplan.c)**: `create_plan()` converts the cheapest selected `Path` tree into an executable `Plan` tree; `set_plan_references()` fixes Var attribute numbers
- Produces **`PlannedStmt*`**

**Stage 5 — Volcano-Style Execution** (`executor/execProcnode.c` + `execMain.c`):

\\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n389: \\\\t}\\\\n390: \\\\n391: \\\\tExecSetExecProcNode(result, result-\\\\u003eExecProcNode);\\\\n392: \\\\n393: \\\\t/*\\\\n394: \\\\t * Initialize any initPlans present in The planner put them in\\\\n395: \\\\t * a separate list for us.\\\\n396: \\\\t *\\\\n397: \\\\t * The defining characteristic of initplans is that they don't have\\\\n398: \\\\t * arguments, so we don't need to evaluate them (in contrast to\\\\n399: \\\\t * ExecInitSubPlanExpr()).\\\\n400: \\\\t */\\\\n401: \\\\tsubps = NIL;\\\\n402: \\\\tforeach(l, node-\\\\u003einitPlan)\\\\n403: \\\\t{\\\\n404: \\\\t\\\\tSubPlan    *subplan = (SubPlan *) lfirst(l);\\\\n405: \\\\t\\\\tSubPlanState *sstate;\\\\n406: \\\\n407: \\\\t\\\\tAssert(IsA(subplan, SubPlan));\\\\n408: \\\\t\\\\tAssert(subplan-\\\\u003eargs == NIL);\\\\n409: \\\\t\\\\tsstate = ExecInitSubPlan(subplan, result);\\\\n410: \\\\t\\\\tsubps = lappend(subps, sstate);\\\\n411: \\\\t}\\\\n412: \\\\tresult-\\\\u003einitPlan = subps;\\\\n413: \\\\n414: \\\\t/* Set up instrumentation for this node if requested */\\\\n415: \\\\tif (estate-\\\\u003ees_instrument)\\\\n416: \\\\t\\\\tresult-\\\\u003einstrument = InstrAlloc(1, estate-\\\\u003ees_instrument,\\\\n417: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult-\\\\u003easync_capable);\\\\n418: \\\\n419: \\\\treturn result;\\\\n420: }\\\\n421: \\\\n422: \\\\n423: /*\\\\n424:  * If a node wants to change its ExecProcNode function after ExecInitNode()\\\\n425:  * has finished, it should do so with this function.  That way any wrapper\\\\n426:  * functions can be reinstalled, without the node having to know how that\\\\n427:  * works.\\\\n428:  */\\\\n429: void\\\\n430: ExecSetExecProcNode(PlanState *node, ExecProcNodeMtd function)\\\\n431: {\\\\n432: \\\\t/*\\\\n433: \\\\t * Add a wrapper around the ExecProcNode callback that checks stack depth\\\\n434: \\\\t * during the first execution and maybe adds an instrumentation wrapper.\\\\n435: \\\\t * When the callback is changed after execution has already begun that\\\\n436: \\\\t * means we'll superfluously execute ExecProcNodeFirst, but that seems ok.\\\\n437: \\\\t */\\\\n438: \\\\tnode-\\\\u003eExecProcNodeReal = function;\\\\n439: \\\\tnode-\\\\u003eExecProcNode = ExecProcNodeFirst;\\\\n440: }\\\\n441: \\\\n442: \\\\n443: /*\\\\n444:  * ExecProcNode wrapper that performs some one-time checks, before calling\\\\n445:  * the relevant node method (possibly via an instrumentation wrapper).\\\\n446:  */\\\\n447: static TupleTableSlot *\\\\n448: ExecProcNodeFirst(PlanState *node)\\\\n449: {\\\\n450: \\\\t/*\\\\n451: \\\\t * Perform stack depth check during the first execution of the node.  We\\\\n452: \\\\t * only do so the first time round because it turns out to not be cheap on\\\\n453: \\\\t * some common architectures (eg. x86).  This relies on the assumption\\\\n454: \\\\t * that ExecProcNode calls for a given plan node will always be made at\\\\n455: \\\\t * roughly the same stack depth.\\\\n456: \\\\t */\\\\n457: \\\\tcheck_stack_depth();\\\\n458: \\\\n459: \\\\t/*\\\\n460: \\\\t * If instrumentation is required, change the wrapper to one that just\\\\n461: \\\\t * does instrumentation.  Otherwise we can dispense with all wrappers and\\\\n462: \\\\t * have ExecProcNode() directly call the relevant function from now on.\\\\n463: \\\\t */\\\\n464: \\\\tif (node-\\\\u003einstrument)\\\\n465: \\\\t\\\\tnode-\\\\u003eExecProcNode = ExecProcNodeInstr;\\\\n466: \\\\telse\\\\n467: \\\\t\\\\tnode-\\\\u003eExecProcNode = node-\\\\u003eExecProcNodeReal;\\\\n468: \\\\n469: \\\\treturn node-\\\\u003eExecProcNode(node);\\\\n470: }\\\\n471: \\\\n472: \\\\n473: /*\\\\n474:  * ExecProcNode wrapper that performs instrumentation calls.  By keeping\\\\n475:  * this a separate function, we avoid overhead in the normal case where\\\\n476:  * no instrumentation is wanted.\\\\n477:  */\\\\n478: static TupleTableSlot *\\\\n479: ExecProcNodeInstr(PlanState *node)\\\\n480: {\\\\n481: \\\\tTupleTableSlot *result;\\\\n482: \\\\n483: \\\\tInstrStartNode(node-\\\\u003einstrument);\\\\n484: \\\\n485: \\\\tresult = node-\\\\u003eExecProcNodeReal(node);\\\\n486: \\\\n487: \\\\tInstrStopNode(node-\\\\u003einstrument, TupIsNull(result) ? 0.0 : 1.0);\\\\n488: \\\\n489: \\\\treturn result;\\\\n490: }\\\\n491: \\\\n492: \\\\n493: /* ----------------------------------------------------------------\\\\n494:  *\\\\t\\\\tMultiExecProcNode\\\\n495:  *\\\\n496:  *\\\\t\\\\tExecute a node that doesn't return individual tuples\\\\n497:  *\\\\t\\\\t(it might return a hashtable, bitmap, etc).  Caller should\\\\n498:  *\\\\t\\\\tcheck it got back the expected kind of Node.\\\\n499:  *\\\\n500:  * This has essentially the same responsibilities as ExecProcNode,\\\\n501:  * but it does not do InstrStartNode/InstrStopNode (mainly because\\\\n502:  * it can't tell how many returned tuples to count).  Each per-node\\\\n503:  * function must provide its own instrumentation support.\\\\n504:  * ----------------------------------------------------------------\\\\n505:  */\\\\n506: Node *\\\\n507: MultiExecProcNode(PlanState *node)\\\\n508: {\\\\n509: \\\\tNode\\\\t   *result;\\\\n510: \\\\n511: \\\\tcheck_stack_depth();\\\\n512: \\\\n513: \\\\tCHECK_FOR_INTERRUPTS();\\\\n514: \\\\n515: \\\\tif (node-\\\\u003echgParam != NULL) /* something changed */\\\\n516: \\\\t\\\\tExecReScan(node);\\\\t\\\\t/* let ReScan handle this */\\\\n517: \\\\n518: \\\\tswitch (nodeTag(node))\\\\n519: \\\\t{\\\\n520: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\t/*\\\\n521: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\t * Only node types that actually support multiexec will be listed\\\\n522: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\t */\\\\n523: \\\\n524: \\\\t\\\\tcase T_HashState:\\\\n525: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult = MultiExecHash((HashState *) node);\\\\n526: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n527: \\\\n528: \\\\t\\\\tcase T_BitmapIndexScanState:\\\\n529: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapIndexScan((BitmapIndexScanState *) node);\\\\n530: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n531: \\\\n532: \\\\t\\\\tcase T_BitmapAndState:\\\\n533: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapAnd((BitmapAndState *) node);\\\\n534: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n535: \\\\n536: \\\\t\\\\tcase T_BitmapOrState:\\\\n537: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapOr((BitmapOrState *) node);\\\\n538: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n539: \\\\n540: \\\\t\\\\tdefault:\\\\n541: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\telog(ERROR, \\\\\\\"unrecognized node type: %d\\\\\\\", (int) nodeTag(node));\\\\n542: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tresult = NULL;\\\\n543: \\\\t\\\\t\\\\tbreak;\\\\n544: \\\\t}\\\\n545: \\\\n546: \\\\treturn result;\\\\n547: }\\\\n548: \\\\n549: \\\\n550: /* ----------------------------------------------------------------\\\\n551:  *\\\\t\\\\tExecEndNode\\\\n552:  *\\\\n553:  *\\\\t\\\\tRecursively cleans up all the nodes in the plan rooted\\\\n554:  *\\\\t\\\\tat 'node'.\\\\n555:  *\\\\n556:  *\\\\t\\\\tAfter this operation, the query plan will not be able to be\\\\n557:  *\\\\t\\\\tprocessed any further. It is now all combined into\\n22:  *\\t\\tone file so that it is easier to keep the dispatch routines\\n23:  *\\t\\tin sync when new nodes are added.\\n24:  *\\n25:  *\\t EXAMPLE\\n26:  *\\t\\tSuppose we want the age of the manager of the shoe department and\\n27:  *\\t\\tthe number of employees in that department.  So we have the query:\\n28:  *\\n29:  *\\t\\t\\t\\tselect DEPT.no_emps, EMP.age\\n30:  *\\t\\t\\t\\tfrom DEPT, EMP\\n31:  *\\t\\t\\t\\twhere EMP.name = DEPT.mgr and\\n32:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t  DEPT.name = \\\"shoe\\\"\\n33:  *\\n34:  *\\t\\tSuppose the planner gives us the following plan:\\n35:  *\\n36:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\tNest Loop (DEPT.mgr = EMP.name)\\n37:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t/\\t\\t\\\\\\n38:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t   /\\t\\t \\\\\\n39:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t   Seq Scan\\t\\tSeq Scan\\n40:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t\\tDEPT\\t\\t  EMP\\n41:  *\\t\\t\\t\\t(name = \\\"shoe\\\")\\n42:  *\\n43:  *\\t\\tExecutorStart() is called first.\\n44:  *\\t\\tIt calls InitPlan() which calls ExecInitNode() on\\n45:  *\\t\\tthe root of the plan -- the nest loop node.\\n46:  *\\n47:  *\\t  * ExecInitNode() notices that it is looking at a nest loop and\\n48:  *\\t\\tas the code below demonstrates, it calls ExecInitNestLoop().\\n49:  *\\t\\tEventually this calls ExecInitNode() on the right and left subplans\\n50:  *\\t\\tand so forth until the entire plan is initialized.  The result\\n51:  *\\t\\tof ExecInitNode() is a plan state tree built with the same structure\\n52:  *\\t\\tas the underlying plan tree.\\n53:  *\\n54:  *\\t  * Then when ExecutorRun() is called, it calls ExecutePlan() which calls\\n55:  *\\t\\tExecProcNode() repeatedly on the top node of the plan state tree.\\n56:  *\\t\\tEach time this happens, ExecProcNode() will end up calling\\n57:  *\\t\\tExecNestLoop(), which calls ExecProcNode() on its subplans.\\n58:  *\\t\\tEach of these subplans is a sequential scan so ExecSeqScan() is\\n59:  *\\t\\tcalled.  The slots returned by ExecSeqScan() may contain\\n60:  *\\t\\ttuples which contain the attributes ExecNestLoop() uses to\\n61:  *\\t\\tform the tuples it returns.\\n62:  *\\n63:  *\\t  * Eventually ExecSeqScan() stops returning tuples and the nest\\n64:  *\\t\\tloop join ends.  Lastly, ExecutorEnd() calls ExecEndNode() which\\n65:  *\\t\\tcalls ExecEndNestLoop() which in turn calls ExecEndNode() on\\n66:  *\\t\\tits subplans which result in ExecEndSeqScan().\\n67:  *\\n68:  *\\t\\tThis should show how the executor works by having\\n69:  *\\t\\tExecInitNode(), ExecProcNode() and ExecEndNode() dispatch\\n70:  *\\t\\ttheir work to the appropriate node support routines which By keeping\\n475:  * this a separate function, we avoid overhead in the normal case where\\n476:  * no instrumentation is wanted.\\n477:  */\\n478: static TupleTableSlot *\\n479: ExecProcNodeInstr(PlanState *node)\\n480: {\\n481: \\tTupleTableSlot *result;\\n482: \\n483: \\tInstrStartNode(node->instrument);\\n484: \\n485: \\tresult = node->ExecProcNodeReal(node);\\n486: \\n487: \\tInstrStopNode(node->instrument, TupIsNull(result) ? 0.0 : 1.0);\\n488: \\n489: \\treturn result;\\n490: }\\n491: \\n492: \\n493: /* ----------------------------------------------------------------\\n494:  *\\t\\tMultiExecProcNode\\n495:  *\\n496:  *\\t\\tExecute a node that doesn't return individual tuples\\n497:  *\\t\\t(it might return a hashtable, bitmap, etc).  Caller should\\n498:  *\\t\\tcheck it got back the expected kind of Node.\\n499:  *\\n500:  * This has essentially the same responsibilities as ExecProcNode,\\n501:  * but it does not do InstrStartNode/InstrStopNode (mainly because\\n502:  * it can't tell how many returned tuples to count).  Each per-node\\n503:  * function must provide its own instrumentation support.\\n504:  * ----------------------------------------------------------------\\n505:  */\\n506: Node *\\n507: MultiExecProcNode(PlanState *node)\\n508: {\\n509: \\tNode\\t   *result;\\n510: \\n511: \\tcheck_stack_depth();\\n512: \\n513: \\tCHECK_FOR_INTERRUPTS();\\n514: \\n515: \\tif (node->chgParam != NULL) /* something changed */\\n516: \\t\\tExecReScan(node);\\t\\t/* let ReScan handle this */\\n517: \\n518: \\tswitch (nodeTag(node))\\n519: \\t{\\n520: \\t\\t\\t/*\\n521: \\t\\t\\t * Only node types that actually support multiexec will be listed\\n522: \\t\\t\\t */\\n523: \\n524: \\t\\tcase T_HashState:\\n525: \\t\\t\\tresult = MultiExecHash((HashState *) node);\\n526: \\t\\t\\tbreak;\\n527: \\n528: \\t\\tcase T_BitmapIndexScanState:\\n529: \\t\\t\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapIndexScan((BitmapIndexScanState *) node);\\n530: \\t\\t\\tbreak;\\n531: \\n532: \\t\\tcase T_BitmapAndState:\\n533: \\t\\t\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapAnd((BitmapAndState *) node);\\n534: \\t\\t\\tbreak;\\n535: \\n536: \\t\\tcase T_BitmapOrState:\\n537: \\t\\t\\tresult = MultiExecBitmapOr((BitmapOrState *) node);\\n538: \\t\\t\\tbreak;\\n539: \\n540: \\t\\tdefault:\\n541: \\t\\t\\telog(ERROR, \\\"unrecognized node type: %d\\\", (int) nodeTag(node));\\n542: \\t\\t\\tresult = NULL;\\n543: \\t\\t\\tbreak;\\n544: \\t}\\n545: \\n546: \\treturn result;\\n547: }\\n548: \\n549: \\n550: /* ----------------------------------------------------------------\\n551:  *\\t\\tExecEndNode\\n552:  *\\n553:  *\\t\\tRecursively cleans up all the nodes in the plan rooted\\n554:  *\\t\\tat 'node'.\\n555:  *\\n556:  *\\t\\tAfter this operation, the query plan will not be able to be\\n557:  *\\t\\tprocessed any further.  This should be called only after\\n558:  *\\t\\tthe query plan has been fully executed.\\n559:  * ----------------------------------------------------------------\\n560:  */\\n561: void\\n562: ExecEndNode(PlanState *node)\\n563: {\\n564: \\t/*\\n565: \\t * do nothing when we get to the end of a leaf on tree.\\n566: \\t */\\n567: \\tif (node == NULL)\\n568: \\t\\treturn;\\n569: \\n570: \\t/*\\n571: \\t * Make sure there's enough stack available. top-down\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Design Patterns Identified\n\n**1. Strict Pipeline (Staged Transformation)**\nPostgreSQL enforces a strict separation between pipeline stages with well-defined intermediate data types. Each stage is isolated: the grammar cannot perform table access (it only produces raw syntax trees), analysis resolves names and types, the rewriter fires rules, the planner optimizes, and the executor runs. This isolation enables robustness: parsing succeeds even inside an aborted transaction (postgres.c comment, line 1062).\n\n**2. Visitor/Dispatch Pattern via Node Tags**\nAll internal data structures are \"tagged nodes\": the first field of every struct is a `NodeTag` enum (e.g., `T_SeqScan`, `T_NestLoop`, `T_Query`). Switch-case dispatch on these tags is the standard idiom throughout:\n- `ExecInitNode()` in execProcnode.c: switch on `nodeTag(plan)` \u2192 calls `ExecInit<NodeType>()`\n- `ExecEndNode()` in execProcnode.c: switch on `nodeTag(planstate)` \u2192 calls `ExecEnd<NodeType>()`\n- `create_plan_recurse()` in createplan.c: switch on `best_path->pathtype` \u2192 calls `create_<type>_plan()`\n- `transformStmt()` in analyze.c: switch on `nodeTag(parseTree)` \u2192 calls `transform<StmtType>()`\n\n**3. Volcano / Iterator Model (Pull-based Execution)**\nThe executor implements the classic Volcano/iterator model (Graefe, 1994). Each plan node exposes a `next()` interface via the `ExecProcNode` function pointer stored in `PlanState`. `ExecutePlan()` drives the computation by repeatedly calling `ExecProcNode(topNode)`. Each node's function pulls tuples from its children, applies its logic (joins, aggregations, sorts, filters), and returns one result tuple at a time in a `TupleTableSlot`. The stream ends when a node returns a NULL or empty slot.\n\nA key implementation refinement: `ExecSetExecProcNode()` installs `ExecProcNodeFirst` as an initial wrapper that performs a one-time stack depth check on the first call, then replaces itself with either the real function or an instrumentation wrapper (`ExecProcNodeInstr`). This avoids overhead on hot paths.\n\n**4. Two-Phase Optimization (Logical/Physical Separation)**\nThe optimizer cleanly separates logical access planning (Phase 1) from physical plan generation (Phase 2):\n\n- **Phase 1 \u2014 Path Generation** (`allpaths.c`, `planmain.c`, `joinpath.c`, `indxpath.c`, `costsize.c`): `make_one_rel()` generates all valid access paths for each relation and join combination. `Path` objects are cost-annotated alternatives (SeqScanPath, IndexPath, NestLoopPath, HashPath, MergePath, etc.). For join ordering, the planner uses dynamic programming (`joinrels.c`; O(2^N) for N relations) or, for large join sets (> `geqo_threshold`), switches to the Genetic Query Optimizer (`geqo/`). `set_cheapest()` selects the minimum-cost path per rel.\n\n- **Phase 2 \u2014 Plan Creation** (`createplan.c`): `create_plan_recurse()` walks the selected Path tree and instantiates corresponding Plan nodes. `Path` is an optimizer-internal structure; `Plan` is the executor-facing structure. The conversion resolves expression formats, builds target lists, and attaches qualification lists. After this, `set_plan_references()` (setrefs.c) resolves Var references to use runtime attribute numbers.\n\n**5. Hook Architecture**\nPostgreSQL provides hook function pointers at all major pipeline boundaries: `planner_hook` (planner.c), `post_parse_analyze_hook` (analyze.c), `ExecutorStart_hook` / `ExecutorRun_hook` / `ExecutorFinish_hook` / `ExecutorEnd_hook` (execMain.c), `set_rel_pathlist_hook` / `join_search_hook` (allpaths.c). This enables extensions (e.g., pg_stat_statements, auto_explain, JIT plugins) to intercept at any stage without modifying core code.\n\n### Component Responsibilities\n\n| Stage | Key File(s) | Input | Output |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| Traffic cop | tcop/postgres.c | query string (char*) | orchestrates pipeline |\n| Lexer | parser/scan.l | query string | token stream |\n| Grammar | parser/gram.y | token stream | List\\<RawStmt*\\> |\n| Semantic analysis | parser/analyze.c | RawStmt* | Query* |\n| Query rewriter | rewrite/rewriteHandler.c | Query* | List\\<Query*\\> |\n| Planner entry | optimizer/plan/planner.c | Query* | PlannedStmt* |\n| Path generation | optimizer/path/allpaths.c | Query / PlannerInfo | RelOptInfo w/ Path alternatives |\n| Plan creation | optimizer/plan/createplan.c | Path* (best) | Plan* tree |\n| Portal / QueryDesc | tcop/pquery.c | PlannedStmt* | QueryDesc* |\n| Executor init | executor/execMain.c + execProcnode.c | QueryDesc* | PlanState* tree |\n| Executor run | executor/execMain.c + execProcnode.c | PlanState* | TupleTableSlot* (per tuple) |\n\n### Data Flow Description\n\n```\nSQL string (char*)\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc raw_parser() [scan.l + gram.y]\nList<RawStmt*>               \u2190 unresolved syntax trees; no catalog access\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc parse_analyze_fixedparams() [analyze.c + parse_*.c]\nQuery*                       \u2190 resolved names, type-checked, catalog-bound\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc QueryRewrite() [rewriteHandler.c]\nList<Query*>                 \u2190 rules/views expanded; may be 1\u2192N (one query \u2192 multiple)\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc planner() [planner.c]\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 subquery_planner() + grouping_planner() + query_planner()\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 make_one_rel() [allpaths.c] \u2192 RelOptInfo w/ Path alternatives + cost estimates\n    \u2502   \u2514\u2500 create_plan() [createplan.c] \u2192 Plan* tree + set_plan_references()\nPlannedStmt*                 \u2190 executor-ready plan; Vars use runtime attribute numbers\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc CreateQueryDesc() + ExecutorStart() [execMain.c + execProcnode.c]\nPlanState* tree              \u2190 per-node runtime state; ExecProcNode fn ptrs installed\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc ExecutorRun() \u2192 ExecutePlan() \u2192 ExecProcNode() [Volcano pull model]\nTupleTableSlot* (repeated)   \u2190 one tuple per call; NULL when exhausted\n    \u2502\n    \u25bc DestReceiver (e.g., printtup \u2192 libpq wire protocol)\nClient receives result rows\n```\n\n### Interface Contracts Between Components\n\n- **Parser \u2192 Analyzer**: `RawStmt*` \u2014 contains the raw `stmt` node (e.g., `SelectStmt*`) and source location. No catalog access performed; syntactically complete but semantically unresolved.\n- **Analyzer \u2192 Rewriter**: `Query*` \u2014 `commandType` (CMD_SELECT/INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE), `rtable` (range table with resolved RangeTblEntry), `jointree` (join tree), `targetList` (target expressions as typed Expr nodes), `havingQual`, etc.\n- **Rewriter \u2192 Planner**: `List<Query*>` \u2014 same Query structure; may have additional queries added by rule firing. Utility statements bypass the planner (returned directly as CMD_UTILITY Query nodes).\n- **Planner \u2192 Executor**: `PlannedStmt*` \u2014 contains `planTree` (root `Plan*`), `rtable` (runtime range table), `commandType`, `subplans`, `rowMarks`, `permInfos`. All Var references use final attribute numbers; `Plan` nodes contain `targetlist`, `qual`, `lefttree`, `righttree`.\n- **Executor internal**: `PlanState*` / `ExecProcNodeMtd` (`typedef TupleTableSlot *(*ExecProcNodeMtd)(PlanState *pstate)`) \u2014 each node returns one tuple per call via `node->ExecProcNode(node)`. Parent nodes drive children by calling their `ExecProcNode`. NULL/empty slot signals end of data.\n\n### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch in execProcnode.c\n\n`ExecInitNode()` (execProcnode.c:142) initializes the entire plan tree **recursively top-down**:\n1. Switches on `nodeTag(plan)` to call the appropriate `ExecInit<Type>()` function.\n2. Each `ExecInit<Type>()` allocates and initializes a `<Type>State` struct (a `PlanState` subtype), sets its `ExecProcNode` to the type's exec function (e.g., `ExecSeqScan`, `ExecNestLoop`), then recursively calls `ExecInitNode()` on its child plans.\n3. After type-specific init, `ExecSetExecProcNode(result, result->ExecProcNode)` installs `ExecProcNodeFirst` as a one-time wrapper for stack-depth checking.\n4. Returns the root `PlanState*` to `InitPlan()` which stores it in `QueryDesc->planstate`.\n\n`ExecProcNode()` (defined as a static inline in executor.h calling `node->ExecProcNode(node)`) is the per-tuple iterator interface:\n- First call goes through `ExecProcNodeFirst()`, which checks stack depth, then installs either `ExecProcNodeInstr` (if instrumentation active) or the real function directly on the node, avoiding wrapper overhead on subsequent calls.\n- Each node's exec function (e.g., `ExecNestLoop`) calls `ExecProcNode(outerPlanState)` and `ExecProcNode(innerPlanState)` to pull tuples from children \u2014 the classic Volcano \"open/next/close\" model flattened to a stateful \"next\" interface.\n- `MultiExecProcNode()` handles nodes that produce bulk outputs rather than individual tuples (Hash, BitmapIndexScan, BitmapAnd/Or) \u2014 these nodes build internal data structures (hash tables, bitmap sets) rather than yielding tuples.\n\n`ExecEndNode()` (execProcnode.c:561) tears down the plan state tree **recursively top-down** with a switch on `nodeTag(planstate)`, freeing resources in each node.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline transforms a SQL string through five staged subsystems: the **parser** (scan.l + gram.y) produces `RawStmt` syntax trees without catalog access; the **semantic analyzer** (analyze.c) resolves names and types against the catalog, producing `Query` trees; the **rule rewriter** (rewriteHandler.c) expands views and rules, yielding a list of `Query` objects; `ExecutorEnd()` \u2192 `ExecEndNode()` cleans up state tree top-down\n   121\t\n   122\t---\n   123\t\n   124\t## Analysis\n   125\t\n   126\t### Design Patterns Identified\n   127\t\n   128\t**1. Strict Pipeline (Staged Transformation)**\n   129\tPostgreSQL enforces a strict separation between pipeline stages with well-defined intermediate data types. Each stage is isolated: the grammar cannot perform table access (it only produces raw syntax trees), analysis resolves names and types, the rewriter fires rules, the planner optimizes, and the executor runs. This isolation enables robustness: parsing succeeds even inside an aborted transaction (postgres.c comment, line 1062).\n   130\t\n   131\t**2. Visitor/Dispatch Pattern via Node Tags**\n   132\tAll internal data structures are \"tagged nodes\": the first field of every struct is a `NodeTag` enum (e.g., `T_SeqScan`, `T_NestLoop`, `T_Query`). Switch-case dispatch on these tags is the standard idiom throughout:\n   133\t- `ExecInitNode()` in execProcnode.c: switch on `nodeTag(plan)` \u2192 calls `ExecInit<NodeType>()`\n   134\t- `ExecEndNode()` in execProcnode.c: switch on `nodeTag(planstate)` \u2192 calls `ExecEnd<NodeType>()`\n   135\t- `create_plan_recurse()` in createplan.c: switch on `best_path->pathtype` \u2192 calls `create_<type>_plan()`\n   136\t- `transformStmt()` in analyze.c: switch on `nodeTag(parseTree)` \u2192 calls `transform<StmtType>()`\n   137\t\n   138\t**3. Volcano / Iterator Model (Pull-based Execution)**\n   139\tThe executor implements the classic Volcano/iterator model (Graefe, 1994). Each plan node exposes a `next()` interface via the `ExecProcNode` function pointer stored in `PlanState`. `ExecutePlan()` drives the computation by repeatedly calling `ExecProcNode(topNode)`. Each node's function pulls tuples from its children, applies its logic (joins, aggregations, sorts, filters), and returns one result tuple at a time in a `TupleTableSlot`. The stream ends when a node returns a NULL or empty slot.\n   140\t\n   141\tA key implementation refinement: `ExecSetExecProcNode()` installs `ExecProcNodeFirst` as an initial wrapper that performs a one-time stack depth check on the first call, then replaces itself with either the real function or an instrumentation wrapper (`ExecProcNodeInstr`). This avoids overhead on hot paths.\n   142\t\n   143\t**4. Two-Phase Optimization (Logical/Physical Separation)**\n   144\tThe optimizer cleanly separates logical access planning (Phase 1) from physical plan generation (Phase 2):\n   145\t\n   146\t- **Phase 1 \u2014 Path Generation** (`allpaths.c`, `planmain.c`, `joinpath.c`, `indxpath.c`, `costsize.c`): `make_one_rel()` generates all valid access paths for each relation and join combination. `Path` objects are cost-annotated alternatives (SeqScanPath, IndexPath, NestLoopPath, HashPath, MergePath, etc.). For join ordering, the planner uses dynamic programming (`joinrels.c`; O(2^N) for N relations) or, for large join sets (> `geqo_threshold`), switches to the Genetic Query Optimizer (`geqo/`). `set_cheapest()` selects the minimum-cost path per rel.\n   147\t\n   148\t- **Phase 2 \u2014 Plan Creation** (`createplan.c`): `create_plan_recurse()` walks the selected Path tree and instantiates corresponding Plan nodes. `Path` is an optimizer-internal structure; `Plan` is the executor-facing structure. The conversion resolves expression formats, builds target lists, and attaches qualification lists. After this, `set_plan_references()` (setrefs.c) resolves Var references to use runtime attribute numbers.\n   149\t\n   150\t**5. Hook Architecture**\n   151\tPostgreSQL provides hook function pointers at all major pipeline boundaries: `planner_hook` (planner.c), `post_parse_analyze_hook` (analyze.c), `ExecutorStart_hook` / `ExecutorRun_hook` / `ExecutorFinish_hook` / `ExecutorEnd_hook` (execMain.c), `set_rel_pathlist_hook` / `join_search_hook` (allpaths.c). "### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch in execProcnode.c\n\n`ExecInitNode()` (execProcnode.c:142) initializes the entire plan tree **recursively top-down**:\n1. Switches on `nodeTag(plan)` to call the appropriate `ExecInit<Type>()` function.\n2. Each `ExecInit<Type>()` allocates and initializes a `<Type>State` struct (a `PlanState` subtype), sets its `ExecProcNode` to the type's exec function (e.g., `ExecSeqScan`, `ExecNestLoop`), then recursively calls `ExecInitNode()` on its child plans.\n3. After type-specific init, `ExecSetExecProcNode(result, result->ExecProcNode)` installs `ExecProcNodeFirst` as a one-time wrapper for stack-depth checking.\n4. Returns the root `PlanState*` to `InitPlan()` which stores it in `QueryDesc->planstate`.\n\n`ExecProcNode()` (defined as a static inline in executor.h calling `node->ExecProcNode(node)`) is the per-tuple iterator interface:\n- First call goes through `ExecProcNodeFirst()`, which checks stack depth, then installs either `ExecProcNodeInstr` (if instrumentation active) or the real function directly on the node, avoiding wrapper overhead on subsequent calls.\n- Each node's exec function (e.g., `ExecNestLoop`) calls `ExecProcNode(outerPlanState)` and `ExecProcNode(innerPlanState)` to pull tuples from children \u2014 the classic Volcano \"open/next/close\" model flattened to a stateful \"next\" interface.\n- `MultiExecProcNode()` handles nodes that produce bulk outputs rather than individual tuples (Hash, BitmapIndexScan, BitmapAnd/Or) \u2014 these nodes build internal data structures (hash tables, bitmap sets) rather than yielding tuples.\n\n`ExecEndNode()` (execProcnode.c:561) tears down the plan state tree **recursively top-down** with a switch on `nodeTag(planstate)`, freeing resources in each node.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's query execution pipeline transforms a SQL string through five staged subsystems: the **parser** (scan.l + gram.y) produces `RawStmt` syntax trees without catalog access; the **semantic analyzer** (analyze.c) resolves names and types against the catalog, producing `Query` trees; the **rule rewriter** (rewriteHandler.c) expands views and rules, yielding a list of `Query` objects; the **planner** (planner.c \u2192 allpaths.c \u2192 createplan.c) performs two-phase optimization \u2014 first generating all cost-annotated access `Path` alternatives (via dynamic programming or GEQO), then converting the cheapest Path to an executable `Plan` tree packaged as `PlannedStmt`; and finally the **Volcano-model executor** (execMain.c + execProcnode.c) initializes a mirror `PlanState` tree at `ExecutorStart`, then pulls tuples one-at-a-time via `ExecProcNode()`'s function-pointer dispatch until exhaustion. The entire pipeline is organized around tagged-node structs with switch-case dispatch, strict data-type boundaries between stages (RawStmt \u2192 Query \u2192 PlannedStmt \u2192 TupleTableSlot), and a rich hook architecture enabling extension without core modification."
